      <br><a id="affa"><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">a</span></sup></a><span class="italic">Key Laboratory of Microelectronics and Energy of Henan Province, Henan Joint International Research Laboratory of New Energy Storage Technology, School of Physics and Electronic Engineering, Xinyang Normal University, Xinyang 464000, P.     </p><div id="art-admin"><span class="italic bold">Received 
      11th March 2020
    </span><span class="bold italic">, Accepted 19th May 2020</span></div><p class="bold italic">First published on 20th May 2020</p><hr><div class="abstract"><h2>Abstract</h2><p>As a two-dimensional layered material with a structure analogous to that of graphene, molybdenum disulfide (MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>) holds great promise in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). However, recent research findings have revealed some disadvantages in two-dimensional (2D) materials such as poor interlayer conductivity and structural instability, resulting in poor rate performance and short cycle life for SIBs. Herein, we designed MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> nanoflowers with an ultra-wide spacing interlayer (W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C) anchored on special double carbon tubes to construct three-dimensional (3D) nanostructures. When tested as an anode material in a SIB, the as-prepared CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C sample achieves high capacities (530 and 230 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at current densities of 0.1 and 2 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, respectively). Density functional theory (DFT) calculations demonstrate that the ultra-wide spacing MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C structure is beneficial for the chemical adsorption of sodium ions and facilitates redox reactions. The wide interlayer spacing and the presence of an intermediate carbon layer provide a rapid diffusion channel for ions and offer a free space for volume expansion of the electrode material.</p></div><hr>
    
      <br><table class="box_border"><tbody><tr><td>
        
          <h2 id="sect291"><span class="a_heading">New concepts</span></h2>
          <span>2D transition metal dichalcogenides (TMDCs) such as MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> with an analogous structure to graphene have received great attention as one of the most promising materials for sodium-ion batteries. We introduce polymer-derived carbon embedded in the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> layer to obtain materials with an ultra-wide spacing MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C structure. Electrochemical tests demonstrate that the expanded interlayer spacing can facilitate the insertion/extraction of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> and the ion transport during the charge and discharge process. In addition, the conductivity of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> is also improved by the presence of interlayer derived carbon, which means that the electron transport rate in electrochemical reactions will be improved. Furthermore, the density functional theory (DFT) calculation of sodium storage on W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C inter-overlapped layers reveals that this structure has significantly improved the adsorption and diffusion of sodium ions, compared with the normal MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>. This work improves the performance of sodium-ion batteries by adjusting the structure of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>, which provides new insights into the structural design of 2D materials.</span>
        
      </td></tr></tbody></table><br>
    
    
      
      <h2 id="sect302"><span class="a_heading">1. Introduction</span></h2>
      <span>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted great interest as a substitute for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs). This is because SIBs have the potential for new possibilities in future development compared to LIBs that have been extensively studied.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit1"><sup><span class="sup_ref">13</span></sup></a> However, the radius of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> (1.02 ) is almost twice that of Li<small><sup>+</sup></small> (0.76 ), the reaction kinetics will be slower and the electrode material will face more severe volume expansion in SIBs.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit4"><sup><span class="sup_ref">46</span></sup></a> 2D transition metal dichalcogenides (TMDCs) such as MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> with a structure analogous to that of graphene have received great attention due to their structure and some special properties in physics and chemistry. As a typical 2D material, the 2H phase MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> consists of three layers, the MoSMo unit in the layer is strongly covalently bonded, and the inter-layer is weak van der Waals interactions.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit7"><sup><span class="sup_ref">79</span></sup></a> The high theoretical-specific capacity (670 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>), and 2D layered structure render MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> a desirable electrode material in SIBs. However, the real application of 2D MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> is limited by some of its intrinsic properties. Firstly, bulk MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> is a semiconductor with a bandgap of approximately 1.29 eV.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit10"><sup><span class="sup_ref">10</span></sup></a> Poor conductivity affects the transport of ions and electrons in MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>. Secondly, thermodynamically, 2D layers are easy to restack along the <span class="italic">c</span>-axis forming a bulk material during sodium insertion and extraction.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit11"><sup><span class="sup_ref">11</span></sup></a> Thirdly, the large volume variation and mechanical stress during sodiation/desodiation will lead to the fall off and pulverization of the active material on the current collector, affecting the cycle stability.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit12"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1214</span></sup></a></span>
      <p class="otherpara">In order to solve the above-mentioned challenges, previous research strategies have mainly focused on the construction of various nanoscale structures of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> and the study of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/carbon composites, such as graphene/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit15"><sup><span class="sup_ref">15,16</span></sup></a> carbon nanotubes (CNTs)/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit17"><sup><span class="sup_ref">17,18</span></sup></a> and porous carbons/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit19"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1921</span></sup></a> In addition, the preparation of few-layered MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> with increased reaction sites and inter-layer spacing to increase the diffusion coefficient of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> can also improve the performance of the battery.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit22"><sup><span class="sup_ref">2225</span></sup></a> For instance, few-layer MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> nanosheets have been successfully synthesized <span class="italic">via</span> a simple and scalable ultrasonic exfoliation technique.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit26"><sup><span class="sup_ref">26</span></sup></a> Due to the intercalation of formamide, the number of layers of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> is greatly reduced, and the interlayer spacing is increased from 0.62 to 0.638 nm. Benefiting from these special structures, the ultrathin MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> nanosheet electrode exhibits a sodium storage capacity of 386 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> after 100 cycles at 40 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small>. Therefore, combining with various carbon materials, expanding the spacing of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> layer could be an effective way to improve the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> storage properties of TMDCs.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">Herein, we report a bottom-up strategy for the synthesis of ultra-wide interlayer spacing MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> flowers (W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C) anchored on special double-layer carbon tubes to construct three-dimensional (3D) nanostructures. This special double-layer carbon tube (CNT@NCT), is manufactured through the sacrificial template method and consists of a hollow nitrogen-doped carbon tube (NCT) nested outside a carbon nanotube (CNT). Subsequently, CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C was fabricated through the <span class="italic">in situ</span> nucleation and growth of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers on polyvinyl pyrrolidone and CNT@NCT. The critical feature of the CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C for boosting SIB performance is the polymer-derived carbon embedded in the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> layer, which facilitates the insertion/extraction of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> and the ion transport during the charge and discharge process. Consequently, the CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C hybrids exhibited higher capacity and excellent cycling stability compared to CNT@NCT@MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> when used as an SIB anode material. Electrochemical characterization and density functional theory (DFT) calculations were performed to demonstrate the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> storage mechanism and the rational design of the conductivity.</p>
    
    
      
      <h2 id="sect355"><span class="a_heading">2. Experimental section</span></h2>
      
        
        <h3 id="sect358"><span class="b_heading">2.1 Sample preparation</span></h3>
        <div>
          
          <span id="sect361"><span class="c_heading_indent">2.1.1 Synthesis of CNT@NCT. </span>
          <span>A mixture solution containing 120 mL of DI water, 36 mL of ethanol and 1.5 mL of ammonia (28 wt%) was vigorously stirred. 0.05 g of acid-treated functionalized CNTs were dispersed into the above mixture. Then 0.6 mL of tetraethyl orthosilicate (TEOS) was added into the mixture dropwise, which was further stirred at room temperature for 6 h. Subsequently, 12 mL of dopamine hydrochloride (DA) aqueous solution (50 mg mL<small><sup>1</sup></small>) was added dropwise into the above solution and kept stirring for 36 h. DA can polymerize to polydopamine (PDA) in ammonia solution. Then, CNT@SiO<small><sub>2</sub></small>@PDA composites were obtained after centrifugation and washed with DI water three times and freeze-dried. The as-prepared CNT@SiO<small><sub>2</sub></small>@PDA were calcined at 800 C for 2 h under an ammonia atmosphere with a heating rate of 2 C min<small><sup>1</sup></small> to transform into CNT@SiO<small><sub>2</sub></small>@NCT. The annealed product was then dispersed in 3 M NaOH solution with stable stirring to remove the SiO<small><sub>2</sub></small>. After an etching time of 10 h, CNT@NCT was harvested by several rinse-centrifugation cycles with DI water, and freeze-dried overnight.</span>
        </span></div>
        <div>
          
          <span id="sect371"><span class="c_heading_indent">2.1.2 Growth of W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C nanoflowers on CNT@NCT. </span>
          <span>In a typical synthesis, a mixture solution containing 60 mL of DI water, 40 mg of CNT@NCT and 0.82 g of polyvinyl pyrrolidone (PVP) was ultrasonicated for 4 h. Then 0.2 g ammonium molybdate and 0.18 g thiourea were added into the mixture under normal stirring. Afterwards, the mixture was transferred into a 100 mL Teflon-lined stainless steel autoclave and kept at 190 C for 18 h. The CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C composites were obtained by centrifugation and washed with DI water and ethanol several times before being freeze-dried overnight. Finally, the as-prepared CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C composites were calcined at 500 C for 3 h under an argon atmosphere with a heating rate of 2 C min<small><sup>1</sup></small>. The control sample CNT@NCT@MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> composites were fabricated without adding PVP.</span>
        </span></div>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect380"><span class="b_heading">2.2 Material characterization</span></h3>
        <span>The crystalline structure and phase of the composites were identified by X-ray diffraction (XRD, D8-Advance (Bruker) automated X-ray diffractometer system) using Cu-K (<span class="italic"></span> = 1.5418 ) radiation at 40 kV and 40 mA, with 2<span class="italic"></span> between 5 and 80 at room temperature. Raman spectroscopy was carried out using an INVIA Raman microprobe (Renishaw Instruments) with a 532 nm laser source, a 50 objective lens and a laser power of 10%. The chemical elements were analyzed by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, PerkinElmer model PHI 5600) with a resolution of 0.30.5 eV from a monochromated aluminum anode X-ray source. The thermogravimetric (TG) analyzer curve was obtained using an STA449F5 (NETZSCH) with 100 mL min<small><sup>1</sup></small> of oxygen flow from 20 C to 800 C at a heating rate of 10 C min<small><sup>1</sup></small>. The morphologies were examined on a field emission scanning electron microscope (FESEM, JEOL S-4800) and a transmission electron microscope (TEM, JEOL JEM-2010).</span>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect388"><span class="b_heading">2.3 Electrochemical measurements</span></h3>
        <span>The slurry was prepared using active material, acetylene black and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) binder at a weight ratio of 7<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">2<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1. The material was mixed and milled after adding <span class="italic">N</span>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP) as a solvent. Next, the obtained slurry was cast onto copper foil substrates and dried at about 80 C overnight. After the organic solvent was evaporated, the electrode film was cut to sheets 14 mm in diameter and then dried at 60 C under vacuum for 12 h. The areal density of active material for each electrode is 0.4 mg cm<small><sup>2</sup></small>. The electrochemical properties of SIBs were measured using 2032-type coin cells, which were prepared using a sodium metal sheet as the counter, with glass fiber membrane (Whatman, GF/D) as the separator. The electrolyte was 1 M NaClO<small><sub>4</sub></small> in EC<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">diethyl carbonate (DEC) (1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1 in volume) with 10 wt% fluoroethylene (FEC). Cyclic voltammetry (CV) was carried out on a VMP3 electrochemical workstation under different scanning speeds. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was performed by applying a sine wave with an amplitude of 0.5 mV over the frequency range of 0.01 Hz100 kHz. Charge/discharge tests were carried out on a Neware battery testing system in the potential range of 0.013.0 V.</span>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect395"><span class="b_heading">2.4 DFT calculations</span></h3>
        <span>All the spin-polarized calculations were performed with the PerdewBurkeErnzerhof (PBE) function within the generalized gradient approximation using the VASP code. The energy cutoff was at 450 eV. The occupancy of the one-electron states was calculated using Gaussian smearing (SIGMA = 0.05 eV). The geometry structure was fully optimized until the Feynman forces on each ion were less than 0.01 eV <small><sup>1</sup></small>. The energy was optimized until the difference between two steps was less than 10<small><sup>5</sup></small> eV. The van der Waals interactions were considered by adopting the dispersion correction by virtue of the DFT-D2 approach. As for periodic interface model construction, to minimize the lattice mismatch between MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> and G, a suitable slab containing a <span class="italic">p</span>(3  3) bilayer-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> and <span class="italic">p</span>(4  4) G sandwich structure was adopted. The vacuum between slabs is 15  to avoid unnecessary interaction. A 3  3  1 <span class="italic">k</span>-points mesh was used during optimizations.</span>
      
    
    
      
      <h2 id="sect406"><span class="a_heading">3. Results and discussion</span></h2>
      <span>
        <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1</a> illustrates the synthetic process of the CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C. Briefly, the CNT@NCT were fabricated by two steps involving SiO<small><sub>2</sub></small> and carbon layer coating on CNTs and the subsequent SiO<small><sub>2</sub></small> layer removal. Then, the CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C composites were obtained through the <span class="italic">in situ</span> nucleation and growth of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers on polyvinyl pyrrolidone molecules and CNT@NCT.</span>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig1"><a href="/image/article/2020/NH/d0nh00152j/d0nh00152j-f1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d0nh00152j-f1.tif" src="/image/article/2020/NH/d0nh00152j/d0nh00152j-f1.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 1 </b> <span id="fig1"><span class="graphic_title">Schematic illustration of the fabrication process of CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">For morphological analysis of CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C, scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) coupled high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) were carried out. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2a and b</a> show the SEM images of CNT@NCT and CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C, respectively. The free space between CNT and NCT can be clearly seen (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2a</a>), which proves the successful synthesis of CNT@NCT. After the growth of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> on the CNT@NCT surface (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2b</a>), we can see that many nanospheres are uniformly distributed on the CNT@NCT surface, and the diameter of the nanospheres is about 100 nm. However, through further investigation by TEM (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2c and d</a>), we found that the morphology of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> is not a nanosphere, but a nanoflower consisting of many MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> layers. The bright and dark contrast difference in the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> nanoflowers reflects the varying degrees of overlap between MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> nanolayers. At the same time, from Fig. S1b and c (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) we can see that although the morphologies of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> and W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C are almost the same from the SEM images, MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> is composed of several dense layers from the TEM images. The HRTEM images of the CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C and CNT@NCT@MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2e and f</a>. By comparing the two pictures, we find that the interlayer spacing of W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C nanoflowers measured from <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2e</a> is about 1.25 nm while the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> nanoflowers have a conventional structure with <span class="italic">d</span>(002) = 0.64 nm (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2f</a>). These results demonstrate that by adding polymer molecules, we have successfully prepared MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>, which has an enlarged interlayer spacing. The expanded interlayer spacing can offer abundant active sites for faradaic redox reactions and reduce the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> diffusion resistance. In addition, the conductivity of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> is also improved by the presence of interlayer derived carbon, which means that the electron transport rate in electrochemical reactions will be improved. Furthermore, we investigated the elemental distribution of CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C, as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2g</a>.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig2"><a href="/image/article/2020/NH/d0nh00152j/d0nh00152j-f2_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d0nh00152j-f2.tif" src="/image/article/2020/NH/d0nh00152j/d0nh00152j-f2.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 2 </b> <span id="fig2"><span class="graphic_title">(a and b) SEM images of the CNT@NCT and CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C. (c and d) TEM images and (e) HRTEM image of CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C. (f) HRTEM image of CNT@NCT@MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>. (g) Corresponding elemental mapping images of CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">The crystalline structures of the CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C, CNT@NCT@MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> and pristine CNT were characterized by XRD. From <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3a</a>, a sharp diffraction peak at 26.1 was found, which should be attributed to the (002) plane of CNTs. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3a</a>, the diffraction peaks of CNT@NCT@MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> can be readily indexed to the hexagonal MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> (JCPDS card No. 37-1492), and the <span class="italic">d</span>(002)-spacing of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> is calculated to be 0.62 nm. For the CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C, the 14.378 peak of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> cannot be found, and two new peaks at 6.438 (*1) and 12.98 (*2) are observed. The disappearance of the 14.378 peak means that the structure of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> in the CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C composites is single or few layers.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit27"><sup><span class="sup_ref">27</span></sup></a> The <span class="italic">d</span>-spacings of peak *1 and *2 were calculated using the Bragg equation, and are approximately 13.52  and 6.68 . The <span class="italic">d</span>-spacing of peak *1 is attributed to the diffraction from the adjacent MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> layers, which indicates that the PVP-derived carbon causes the expansion of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> sheets.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit28"><sup><span class="sup_ref">28,29</span></sup></a> The <span class="italic">d</span>-spacing of peak *2 is about 6.68 , which is related to the spacing between the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> layer and PVP-derived carbon layer, and it is exactly half of the spacing of the two MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> layers. This result is in accordance with the TEM image.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig3"><a href="/image/article/2020/NH/d0nh00152j/d0nh00152j-f3_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d0nh00152j-f3.tif" src="/image/article/2020/NH/d0nh00152j/d0nh00152j-f3.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 3 </b> <span id="fig3"><span class="graphic_title">(a) XRD patterns of the CNT, CNT@NCT@MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> and CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C. (b and c) Raman spectrum of the CNT@NCT@MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> and CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C. (d) TGA curves of CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">As displayed in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3b</a>, two peaks appearing at 1346 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small> (the D band) and 1581 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small> (the G band) correspond to carbon. The characteristic peaks at 378 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small> and 403 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small> correspond to the in-plane vibration of Mo and S atoms (E<small><sup>1</sup></small><small><sub>2g</sub></small>) and the out-of-plane vibration of S atoms (A<small><sub>1g</sub></small>), respectively. In addition, it can be seen from <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3c</a> that the differences (<span class="italic">k</span>) between the E<small><sup>1</sup></small><small><sub>2g</sub></small> and A<small><sub>1g</sub></small> peaks of the CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C and CNT@NCT@MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> are 25.08 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small> and 29.70 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small>, respectively. The <span class="italic">k</span> is closely related to the layer number and the thickness of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>, and it increases as the layer number increases.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit30"><sup><span class="sup_ref">30,31</span></sup></a> Therefore, we can conclude that W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C not only has a larger interlayer spacing but also a lower number of layers than conventional MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>. To further prove that PVP-derived carbon exists between the layer of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> nanosheets, we performed Raman testing for W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C and pure MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>. As displayed in Fig. S2 (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>), both W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C and pure MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> have characteristic peaks of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>, and only W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C has characteristic peaks of carbon. Thus, we can conclude that there is PVP-derived carbon in W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C. To check the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> content in the CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C composites, the CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C, W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C and pure MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> were characterized by thermogravimetric (TG) analysis in an oxygen atmosphere. From the TG analysis (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3d</a>), it can be calculated that the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> content is 42.89% (Please check the calculation details in the ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). The typical XPS survey spectrum reveals that the CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C mainly consists of Mo, S, C, and N elements (Fig. S3a, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). The corresponding elemental compositions are given in Table S1 (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). As observed in Fig. S3c (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>), the N 1s XPS peaks can be deconvoluted into four peaks at 394.8, 398.2, 400.3, and 401.5 eV, indexing to Mo 2p<small><sub>3/2</sub></small>, pyridinic-N, pyrrolic-N, and graphitic-N, respectively.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit30"><sup><span class="sup_ref">30</span></sup></a> In the Mo 3d spectrum (Fig. S3d, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>), the peaks located at 228.9, 232.1, and 235.3 eV correspond with Mo 3d<small><sub>5/2</sub></small>, Mo 3d<small><sub>3/2</sub></small>, and Mo<small><sup>6+</sup></small>, respectively, while the peak at 226.1 eV is indexed to S 2s.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit32"><sup><span class="sup_ref">32</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">To explore the potential of the 3D CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C composites as anode materials for SIBs, the electrochemical properties were evaluated by using coin-type cells in a voltage range of 0.013.0 V. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4a</a> shows the first, second, and third cyclic voltammetry (CV) cycles of the 3D CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C composites at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<small><sup>1</sup></small> between 0.01 V and 3.0 V <span class="italic">versus</span> Na/Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>. For the initial cathodic scan, a sharp peak and three small peaks can be observed around 0.02, 0.23, 0.5 and 1.2 V, respectively. The peaks around 0.5 and 1.2 V correspond to the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> insertion into MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> (<a title="" href="#eqn1">eqn (1)</a>) and the formation of solidelectrolyte interface (SEI), respectively. The sharp peak around 0.23 V was ascribed to the conversion-type reaction accompanied by the formation of metallic (Mo) and Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>S (<a title="" href="#eqn2">eqn (2)</a>).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit33"><sup><span class="sup_ref">33</span></sup></a> The last small cathodic peak at around 0.02 V can be associated with the intercalation of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> into the carbon.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit34"><sup><span class="sup_ref">34</span></sup></a> In the anodic scan, a small peak at 0.1 V was ascribed to the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> extraction from carbon and a broad oxidation peak was observed at 1.79 V attributed to the oxidation of metallic Mo to MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>. Compared to the next two cycles, the irreversible peaks correspond to the decomposition of electrolyte to form SEI films, resulting in irreversible capacity loss. The electrochemical performance of the CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C electrode was further investigated by galvanostatic charge/discharge measurements. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4b</a>, the CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C exhibits initial discharge and charge capacities of 872 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> and 530 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, corresponding to a coulombic efficiency of 60.8%. The irreversible capacity in the first cycle can be mainly attributed to the formation of the SEI layer.</p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn1"><span id="eqn1">MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> + <span class="italic">x</span>Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> + <span class="italic">x</span>e<small><sup></sup></small>  Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small></span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(1)</td></tr></tbody></table><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn2"><span id="eqn2">Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> + (4  <span class="italic">x</span>)Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> + (4  <span class="italic">x</span>)e<small><sup></sup></small>  Mo + 2Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>S</span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(2)</td></tr></tbody></table>In order to demonstrate the impact of the expanding MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> layers, the rate and long-term cycle performances of the two samples at various current densities were investigated in the 0.013.0 V range (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4c and d</a>). It is remarkable that with an increase of the current density, the CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C exhibited excellent rate capability with reversible capacities of 530 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at 0.1 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small> and 230 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at 2.0 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, respectively. In the case of the CNT@NCT@MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>, the low capacities of 320, 21, and 0.1 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> can be delivered at the current densities of 0.1, 1.0, and 2.0 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, respectively. Moreover, when the current density increases again to 0.1 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, the capacity of CNT@NCT@MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> has a significant decrease trend. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4d</a> shows that the CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C electrode maintains a large discharge capacity of 256 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> after 200 cycles. While, the capacity delivered by the CNT@NCT@MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> electrode dropped sharply and only retained 90 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> after 136 cycles. The W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C offers an effective buffer for accommodating volume changes and alleviating the volume expansion-induced mechanical stresses in the electrode material, resulting in greatly improved cycle performance. The capabilities of the previously reported MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-based electrode materials with long cycling stability for SIBs are compared in Table S2 (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). The morphology of the CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C after 200 cycles at 1 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small> was also studied by SEM (Fig. S6, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). The W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C nanoflowers still hold together with the CNT@NCT, indicating its high structural stability.<p></p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig4"><a href="/image/article/2020/NH/d0nh00152j/d0nh00152j-f4_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d0nh00152j-f4.tif" src="/image/article/2020/NH/d0nh00152j/d0nh00152j-f4.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 4 </b> <span id="fig4"><span class="graphic_title">(a and b) Typical CV curves and GCD profiles of the CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C cathode. (c) Rate performances of the CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C and CNT@NCT@MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> cathodes at different current densities. (d) Cycle performance of the CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C and CNT@NCT@MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> cathodes at a current density of 1 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small> for 200 charge/discharge cycles. (e) Nyquist plots of the CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C and CNT@NCT@MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> cathodes. (f) <span class="italic">b</span> Values determined by the plot of log(<span class="italic">i</span>) <span class="italic">vs.</span> log(<span class="italic"></span>). (g) Normalized contribution ratio of capacitive capacities at different scan rates. (h) Capacitive contribution at 0.3 mV s<small><sup>1</sup></small>.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">To further demonstrate the role of the derived carbon layer in the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> layer, the electrochemical impedance spectra (EIS) measurements of the CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C and CNT@NCT@MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> were performed at open-circuit voltage in a frequency range from 100 kHz to 0.01 Hz at 5 mV amplitude (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4e</a>). The diameter of the semicircle in the high-middle frequency region corresponds to the charge transfer resistance (<span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>ct</sub></small>) at the electrodeelectrolyte interface. The CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C electrode shows a smaller diameter, reflecting faster charge transfer and higher conductivity than the CNT@NCT@MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> electrode.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit35"><sup><span class="sup_ref">35</span></sup></a> This is due to the insertion of the PVP-derived carbon expanding the interlayer spacing of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>, thereby increasing the contact area between the electrode material and the electrolyte, and providing a large number of active sites for charge transfer. In the low-frequency region of the Nyquist plots, the CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C electrode also shows a larger slope than the CNT@NCT@MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>, relating to smaller Warburg impedance (<span class="italic">Z</span><small><sub>w</sub></small>). Small <span class="italic">Z</span><small><sub>w</sub></small> means that the CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C electrode has a faster Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> diffusion rate, which is due to the expanding interlayer and the 3D nanostructure.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">The galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of the CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C show a continuous slope without exhibiting a distinct plateau region, which is a feature of charging/discharging of capacitors. To evaluate the contribution of capacitive charge storage, its ratio was calculated based on the CV data. Fig. S5 (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) shows the CV curves of CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C at scan rates from 0.1 to 1.0 mV s<small><sup>1</sup></small>. The relationship (<span class="italic">i</span> = <span class="italic">a</span><small><sup><span class="italic">b</span></sup></small>, where <span class="italic">a</span> and <span class="italic">b</span> are appropriate parameters) between the peak current (<span class="italic">i</span>) and the sweep rate (<span class="italic"></span>) could be revealed by the power law. The <span class="italic">b</span> value approaches 0.5 corresponding to the diffusion-controlled process, whereas 1 suggests a surface-controlled process. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4f</a>, the <span class="italic">b</span> values for anodic and cathodic peaks were determined as 0.85 and 0.94, respectively. This implies that the surface-controlled process dominates during an electrochemical reaction. More specifically, the ratio of capacitive contribution can be quantitatively analyzed through the method proposed by Dunn:<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit36"><sup><span class="sup_ref">36</span></sup></a><br></p><table><tbody><tr><td class="eqnText"><span class="italic">i</span> = <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>1</sub></small><span class="italic"></span> + <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>1</sub></small><span class="italic"></span><small><sup>1/2</sup></small></td></tr></tbody></table>where <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>1</sub></small> and <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>2</sub></small> are appropriate values, and <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>1</sub></small><span class="italic"></span> and <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>2</sub></small><span class="italic"></span><small><sup>1/2</sup></small> correspond to the contribution of the current response surface-controlled behavior and the diffusion-controlled process, respectively.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit37"><sup><span class="sup_ref">37,38</span></sup></a> When the sweep rate is increased, the charge storage speed of the surface-controlled process will not be affected as much as the diffusion-controlled process. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4g</a>, the role of the capacitive contribution is positively correlated with the sweep rate and it is even as high as 95.3% at a scan rate of 1 mV s<small><sup>1</sup></small>. This is because the pseudocapacitive contribution plays a critical role. The small particle size and few layers of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> and amorphous PVP-derived carbon provide various active redox sites for the pseudocapacitive behavior, which is crucial for the pseudocapacitive reaction.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit39"><sup><span class="sup_ref">39,40</span></sup></a><p></p>
      <p class="otherpara">To better understand the positive influences of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> with expanded interlayer spacing and derived carbon for Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> storage, extensive density functional theory (DFT) calculations were implemented <span class="italic">via</span> a periodic slab model. Since the derived carbon layer obtained from PVP is not well defined, it is difficult to construct an appropriate model. Based on previous literature related to TMD/C,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit28"><sup><span class="sup_ref">28,4145</span></sup></a> we simplified our carbon layer to graphene. We calculated the formation energy of intercalating graphene into the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> layers (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5a</a>). The energy is calculated to be 0.046 eV, based on <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>form</sub></small> = <span class="italic">E</span>(MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/G/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>)  <span class="italic">E</span>(MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>)  <span class="italic">E</span>(G), indicating the thermodynamic stability of derived carbon in the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> interlayer space. Subsequently, we clarified the adsorption sites of Na on monolayer MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>, which verified an active hollow site with <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ad</sub></small> = <span class="italic">E</span>(Na-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>)  <span class="italic">E</span>(Na)  <span class="italic">E</span>(MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>) = 0.734 eV (Fig. S7 and Table S3, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). The Na adsorption on the graphene has been calculated for three possibilities, respectively, <span class="italic">i.e.</span>, on the top, bridge and hollow site. Comparing the adsorption energies on the three sites, we identified that Na is more inclined to adsorb on the bridge site with <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ad</sub></small> at 0.999 eV relative to the C top (0.937 eV) and hollow sites (0.971 eV) (Fig. S7 and Table S3, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). Therefore, we could distinguish that the C atoms exhibited stronger adsorption for Na with a more negative adsorption energy, indicating that the W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C may enhance Na intercalation kinetically (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5b</a>).</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig5"><a href="/image/article/2020/NH/d0nh00152j/d0nh00152j-f5_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d0nh00152j-f5.tif" src="/image/article/2020/NH/d0nh00152j/d0nh00152j-f5.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 5 </b> <span id="fig5"><span class="graphic_title">(a and b) Sodium adsorption in the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> and MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> interlayer, respectively. (c) Relationship between adsorption energy and distance of the sodium ion from the carbon layer. (d) The energy barrier of Na atoms in three different distances; the inset is the 3D contour plot of charge density difference in Na adsorption at the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C interface, in which yellow indicates electron accumulation and light blue for depletion.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">In the expanded bilayer-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>, the intercalating carbon atoms were simply simulated as a graphene layer. We derived that carbon atoms may grasp the Na which signified a positive synthetic-effect on the adsorption of Na. We investigated the Na adsorption energy dependence on the distance between MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> and the carbon layer (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5c</a>). Referring to the carbon layer, we considered three adsorption sites, <span class="italic">i.e.</span>, the top, bridge and hollow site. The bridge site was confirmed as an energy optimum location with <span class="italic">h</span> = 2.52 . The energy difference between the three sites constructed an energy barrier for Na transport. Once the Na moved close to the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> or graphene, the energy barrier was promoted. Evidently, the strong interaction near the layer impeded Na transport. Nevertheless, at the energy optimum site, the energy barrier reached the minimum value (<span class="italic">E</span> = 0.062 eV), which means the Na could transport without noticeable hindrance at this height (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5d</a>). Furthermore, we determined the difference charge density to analyze the chemical bonding. In the bridge configuration, it is easy to find that the charges are mainly lost from the Na and accumulate in the NaS and NaC bonds. Then, Na forms  bonds with the top S atoms and  bonds with the bottom C atoms (inset of <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5d</a>). These chemical bonds are critical for the charge transporting from the Na to MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> layers and carbon layers. Thus, the intercalated carbon atoms (graphene in this model) could create a channel for the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> transport, simultaneously, with an energy transferring process through the chemical bonding (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6</a>).</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig6"><a href="/image/article/2020/NH/d0nh00152j/d0nh00152j-f6_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d0nh00152j-f6.tif" src="/image/article/2020/NH/d0nh00152j/d0nh00152j-f6.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 6 </b> <span id="fig6"><span class="graphic_title">Schematic illustration of the sodium storage mechanism of the electrode.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
    
    
      
      <h2 id="sect735"><span class="a_heading">4. Conclusions</span></h2>
      <span>In summary, we have designed and synthesized a special 3D hybrid nanostructure consisting of 2D ultra-wide spacing MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C inter-overlapped layers and 1D double-layer carbon tubes as the substrate. The 2D MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C inter-overlapped layer spacing is about twice that of the common MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> layer, and there is a layer of derived carbon between the layers. The wide interlayer spacing and the presence of the derived carbon layer provide a rapid diffusion channel for the diffusion of ions, as well as sufficient ion adsorption sites. At the same time, a good contact can be formed between the carbon nanosheet and the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> layer, which improves the conductivity of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>. As a result, the CNT@NCT@W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C electrode delivered a discharge capacity of 530 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at 0.05 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>. After 200 cycles at a high current density of 1 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, it can still maintain a capacity of 256 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>. The DFT calculations of sodium storage on the W-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/C inter-overlapped layers further reveals that this structure has significantly improved the adsorption and diffusion of sodium ions, compared with the normal MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>. This work reveals the importance of increasing the interlayer spacing and the presence of interlayer carbon layers to improve the sodium storage performance of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> electrode, which provides new insights into the structural design of 2D materials for battery electrode materials.</span>

They are usually only set in response to actions made by you which amount to a request for services, such as setting your privacy preferences, logging in or filling in forms. You can set your browser to block or alert you about these cookies, but some parts of the site will not then work. These cookies do not store any personally identifiable information.</p></div></div><div class="ot-accordion-layout ot-cat-item ot-vs-config" data-optanongroupid="C0002"><button aria-expanded="false" ot-accordion="true" aria-controls="ot-desc-id-C0002" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0002"></button><!-- Accordion header --><div class="ot-acc-hdr"><div class="ot-plus-minus"><span></span><span></span></div><h4 class="ot-cat-header" id="ot-header-id-C0002">Analytical cookies</h4><div class="ot-tgl"><input type="checkbox" name="ot-group-id-C0002" id="ot-group-id-C0002" role="switch" class="category-switch-handler" data-optanongroupid="C0002" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0002"> <label class="ot-switch" for="ot-group-id-C0002"><span class="ot-switch-nob"></span> <span class="ot-label-txt">Analytical cookies</span></label> </div></div><!-- accordion detail --><div class="ot-acc-grpcntr ot-acc-txt"><p class="ot-acc-grpdesc ot-category-desc" id="ot-desc-id-C0002">These cookies allow us to count visits and traffic sources so we can measure and improve the performance of our site. They help us to know which pages are the most and least popular and see how visitors move around the site. All information these cookies collect is aggregated and therefore anonymous.
